      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">biological family</a> <b>Canidae</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/: primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/i?/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">i?</span></span>/</a></span></span> <sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> (from Latin, canis, “dog”) is a lineage of <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivorans</a> that includes <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">domestic dogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">wolves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coyotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Coyotes">coyotes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackal" title="Jackal">jackals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dingo" title="Dingo">dingoes</a>, and many other <a href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a> dog-like mammals. A member of this family is called a <b>canid</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/: primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/: primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/e?/: 'a' in 'face'">e?</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/?/: 'i' in 'kit'">?</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The cat-like <a href="/wiki/Feliformia" title="Feliformia">feliforms</a> and dog-like <a href="/wiki/Caniformia" title="Caniformia">caniforms</a> emerged within the <a href="/wiki/Carnivoramorpha" title="Carnivoramorpha">Carnivoramorpha</a> 43 million years before present.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The caniforms included the fox-like genus <i><a href="/wiki/Leptocyon" title="Leptocyon">Leptocyon</a></i> whose various species existed from 34 million years ago (Mya) before branching 11.9 Mya into <a href="/wiki/Vulpes" title="Vulpes">Vulpini</a> (foxes) and <a href="/wiki/Canis" title="Canis">Canini</a> (canines).<sup id="cite_ref-tedford2009_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tedford2009-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>174–5</span></sup> </p>
      <p>Canids are found on all continents except <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, having arrived independently or accompanied human beings over extended periods of time. Canids vary in size from the 2-m-long (6&nbsp;ft 7 in) <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">gray wolf</a> to the 24-cm-long (9.4&nbsp;in) <a href="/wiki/Fennec_fox" title="Fennec fox">fennec fox</a>. The body forms of canids are similar, typically having long muzzles, upright ears, teeth adapted for cracking bones and slicing flesh, long legs, and bushy tails. They are mostly <a href="/wiki/Social_animals" class="mw-redirect" title="Social animals">social animals</a>, living together in family units or small groups and behaving co-operatively. Typically, only the dominant pair in a group breeds, and a litter of young is reared annually in an underground den. Canids communicate by scent signals and vocalizations. They are very intelligent. One canid, the domestic dog, long ago entered into a partnership with humans and today remains one of the most widely kept domestic animals. </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Characteristics"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext"><span>Characteristics</span></span></a> 
         <ul> 
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Dentition"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Dentition</span></a></li> 
         </ul> </li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Life_history"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Life history</span></a>

         </ul> </li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Canids_and_humans"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Canids and humans</span></a></li>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>In the history of the carnivores, the family Canidae is represented by the two extinct <a href="/wiki/Subfamily" title="Subfamily">subfamilies</a> designated as <a href="/wiki/Hesperocyoninae" title="Hesperocyoninae">Hesperocyoninae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Borophaginae" title="Borophaginae">Borophaginae</a>, and the extant subfamily <a href="/wiki/Caninae" title="Caninae">Caninae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-miklosi2015_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miklosi2015-7">[7]</a></sup> This subfamily includes all living canids and their most recent fossil relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-tedford2009_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tedford2009-6">[6]</a></sup> All living canids as a group form a dental <a href="/wiki/Monophyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> relationship with the extinct borophagines, with both groups having a <a href="/wiki/Bicuspid" class="mw-redirect" title="Bicuspid">bicuspid</a> (two points) on the lower <a href="/wiki/Carnassial" title="Carnassial">carnassial</a> <a href="/wiki/Talonid" class="mw-redirect" title="Talonid">talonid</a>, which gives this tooth an additional ability in <a href="/wiki/Mastication" class="mw-redirect" title="Mastication">mastication</a>. This, together with the development of a distinct <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_mammalian_dental_topography#Lower_teeth" title="Glossary of mammalian dental topography">entoconid</a> cusp and the broadening of the talonid of the first lower <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molar</a>, and the corresponding enlargement of the talon of the upper first molar and reduction of its <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_mammalian_dental_topography#Upper_teeth" title="Glossary of mammalian dental topography">parastyle</a> distinguish these late <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> canids and are the essential differences that identify their clade.<sup id="cite_ref-tedford2009_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tedford2009-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>p6</span></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogenetic_relationships">Phylogenetic relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Phylogenetic relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg/220px-Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg/330px-Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg/440px-Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2732" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Indian_Wolf_Photo_Dhaval_Vargiya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Indian wolf at Velavadar (Blackbuck National Park, Gujarat)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes,_Nyctereutes,_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes%2C_Nyctereutes%2C_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png/220px-MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes%2C_Nyctereutes%2C_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes%2C_Nyctereutes%2C_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png/330px-MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes%2C_Nyctereutes%2C_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes%2C_Nyctereutes%2C_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png/440px-MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes%2C_Nyctereutes%2C_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png 2x" data-file-width="802" data-file-height="1153"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P1a_-_Vulpes,_Nyctereutes,_Cuon_%26_Canis_skulls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Skulls of various canid genera; 
         <i><a href="/wiki/Vulpes" title="Vulpes">Vulpes</a></i> (
         <a href="/wiki/Corsac_fox" title="Corsac fox">corsac fox</a>), 
         <i><a href="/wiki/Nyctereutes" title="Nyctereutes">Nyctereutes</a></i> (
         <a href="/wiki/Raccoon_dog" title="Raccoon dog">raccoon dog</a>), 
         <i><a href="/wiki/Cuon" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuon">Cuon</a></i> (
         <a href="/wiki/Dhole" title="Dhole">dhole</a>), and 
         <i><a href="/wiki/Canis" title="Canis">Canis</a></i> (
         <a href="/wiki/Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal">golden jackal</a>)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Within the Canidae, the results of <a href="/wiki/Allozyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Allozyme">allozyme</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosome</a> analyses have previously suggested several <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> divisions: </p> 
      <ol>
       <li>The wolf-like canids (genus <i>Canis</i>, <i>Cuon</i>, and <i>Lycaon</i>) include the <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">domestic dog</a> <i>(Canis lupus familiaris)</i>, <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">gray wolf</a> (<i>Canis lupus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red_wolf" title="Red wolf">red wolf</a> (<i>Canis rufus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eastern_wolf" title="Eastern wolf">eastern wolf</a> (<i>Canis lycaon</i>), <a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">coyote</a> (<i>Canis latrans</i>), <a href="/wiki/Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal">Eurasian golden jackal</a> (<i>Canis aureus</i>), <a href="/wiki/African_golden_wolf" title="African golden wolf">African golden wolf</a> (<i>Canis anthus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_wolf" title="Ethiopian wolf">Ethiopian wolf</a> (<i>Canis simensis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black-backed_jackal" title="Black-backed jackal">black-backed jackal</a> (<i>Canis mesomelas</i>), <a href="/wiki/Side-striped_jackal" title="Side-striped jackal">side-striped jackal</a> (<i>Canis adustus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Dhole" title="Dhole">dhole</a> (<i>Cuon alpinus</i>), and <a href="/wiki/African_wild_dog" title="African wild dog">African wild dog</a> (<i>Lycaon pictus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-wayne1993_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wayne1993-8">[8]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>The fox-like canids include the <a href="/wiki/Kit_fox" title="Kit fox">kit fox</a> (<i>Vulpes velox</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red fox</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>), <a href="/wiki/Cape_fox" title="Cape fox">Cape fox</a> (<i>Vulpes chama</i>), <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a> (<i>Vulpes lagopus</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Fennec_fox" title="Fennec fox">fennec fox</a> (<i>Vulpes zerda</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-wayne1993_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wayne1993-8">[8]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>The South American canids include the <a href="/wiki/Bush_dog" title="Bush dog">bush dog</a> (<i>Speothos venaticus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Hoary_fox" title="Hoary fox">hoary fox</a> (<i>Lycalopex uetulus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Crab-eating_fox" title="Crab-eating fox">crab-eating fox</a> (<i>Cerdocyon thous</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Maned_wolf" title="Maned wolf">maned wolf</a> (<i>Chrysocyon brachyurus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-wayne1993_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wayne1993-8">[8]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>Various <a href="/wiki/Monotypic_taxon" title="Monotypic taxon">monotypic taxa</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Bat-eared_fox" title="Bat-eared fox">bat-eared fox</a> (<i>Otocyon megalotis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Gray_fox" title="Gray fox">gray fox</a> (<i>Urocyon cinereoargenteus</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Raccoon_dog" title="Raccoon dog">raccoon dog</a> (<i>Nyctereutes procyonoides</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-wayne1993_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wayne1993-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
      </ol> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> analysis shows that the first three form <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">clades</a>. The wolf-like canids and the South American canids together form the tribe Canini.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Molecular data imply a North American origin of living Canidae some 10 Mya and an African origin of wolf-like canines (<i>Canis</i>, <i>Cuon</i>, and <i>Lycaon</i>), with the jackals being the most <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> of this group. The South American clade is rooted by the maned wolf and bush dog, and the fox-like canids by the fennec fox and Blanford's fox. The gray fox and island fox are basal to the other clades; however, this topological difference is not strongly supported.<sup id="cite_ref-Lindblad-toh2005_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindblad-toh2005-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The cladogram below is based on the phylogeny of Lindblad-Toh et al. (2005),<sup id="cite_ref-Lindblad-toh2005_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindblad-toh2005-10">[10]</a></sup> modified to incorporate recent findings on <i>Canis</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Koepfli-2015_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koepfli-2015-11">[11]</a></sup> <i>Vulpes</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-ZhaoZhang2016_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZhaoZhang2016-12">[12]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Lycalopex" class="mw-redirect" title="Lycalopex">Lycalopex</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dusicyon" title="Dusicyon">Dusicyon</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> species. </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047358">.mw-parser-output table.clade{border-spacing:0;margin:0;font-size:100%;line-height:100%;border-collapse:separate;width:auto}.mw-parser-output table.clade table.clade{width:100%}.mw-parser-output table.clade td{border:0;padding:0;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label{width:0.8em;border:0;padding:0 0.2em;vertical-align:bottom;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel{border:0;padding:0 0.2em;vertical-align:top;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar{vertical-align:middle;text-align:left;padding:0 0.5em}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf{border:0;padding:0;text-align:left;vertical-align:middle}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leafR{border:0;padding:0;text-align:right}</style>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The Canidae today include a diverse group of some 34 species ranging in size from the maned wolf with its long limbs to the short-legged bush dog. Modern canids inhabit forests, tundra, savannahs, and deserts throughout tropical and temperate parts of the world. The evolutionary relationships between the species have been studied in the past using <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> approaches, but more recently, molecular studies have enabled the investigation of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic relationships</a>. In some species, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_divergence" title="Genetic divergence">genetic divergence</a> has been suppressed by the high level of <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> between different populations and where the species have hybridized, large <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_zone" title="Hybrid zone">hybrid zones</a> exist.<sup id="cite_ref-Wayne_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wayne-15">[15]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eocene_epoch">Eocene epoch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Eocene epoch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Carnivoran" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnivoran">Carnivorans</a> evolved from <a href="/wiki/Miacoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Miacoid">miacoids</a> about 55 Mya during the late <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Some 5 million years later, the carnivorans split into two main divisions: <a href="/wiki/Caniforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Caniforms">caniforms</a> (dog-like) and <a href="/wiki/Feliforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Feliforms">feliforms</a> (cat-like). By 40 Mya, the first member of the dog family proper had arisen. Called <i><a href="/wiki/Prohesperocyon_wilsoni" class="mw-redirect" title="Prohesperocyon wilsoni">Prohesperocyon wilsoni</a></i>, its fossilized remains have been found in what is now the southwestern part of Texas. The chief features which identify it as a canid include the loss of the upper third molar (part of a trend toward a more shearing bite), and the structure of the middle ear which has an enlarged <a href="/wiki/Auditory_bulla" class="mw-redirect" title="Auditory bulla">bulla</a> (the hollow bony structure protecting the delicate parts of the ear). <i>Prohesperocyon</i> probably had slightly longer limbs than its predecessors, and also had parallel and closely touching toes which differ markedly from the splayed arrangements of the digits in <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wang-Tedford_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-Tedford-17">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The canid family soon subdivided into three subfamilies, each of which diverged during the Eocene: <a href="/wiki/Hesperocyoninae" title="Hesperocyoninae">Hesperocyoninae</a> (about 39.74–15 Mya), <a href="/wiki/Borophaginae" title="Borophaginae">Borophaginae</a> (about 34–2 Mya), and <a href="/wiki/Caninae" title="Caninae">Caninae</a> (about 34–0 Mya). The Caninae are the only surviving subfamily and all present-day canids, including wolves, foxes, coyotes, jackals, and domestic dogs, belong to it. Members of each subfamily showed an <a href="/wiki/Cope%27s_rule" title="Cope's rule">increase in body mass with time</a> and some exhibited specialized <a href="/wiki/Hypercarnivorous" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypercarnivorous">hypercarnivorous</a> diets that made them prone to extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>Fig. 1</span></sup> </p> 
      <div style="clear:right;"></div> 
      <div id="Container" class="toccolours searchaux" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">
       <div id="Title" style="background-color:rgb(246,236,57); font-weight:bold; padding:0.2em; text-align:center; margin:0.6em 0 1.2em 0">

         <div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:20em;">
          <div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;">

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oligocene_epoch">Oligocene epoch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Oligocene epoch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>By the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>, all three subfamilies of canids (Hesperocyoninae, Borophaginae, and Caninae) had appeared in the fossil records of North America. The earliest and most primitive branch of the Canidae was the Hesperocyoninae lineage, which included the coyote-sized <i><a href="/wiki/Mesocyon" title="Mesocyon">Mesocyon</a></i> of the Oligocene (38–24 Mya). These early canids probably evolved for the fast pursuit of prey in a grassland habitat; they resembled modern <a href="/wiki/Civet" title="Civet">civets</a> in appearance. Hesperocyonines eventually became extinct in the middle Miocene. One of the early members of the Hesperocyonines, the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Hesperocyon" title="Hesperocyon">Hesperocyon</a></i>, gave rise to <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeocyon" title="Archaeocyon">Archaeocyon</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Leptocyon" title="Leptocyon">Leptocyon</a></i>. These branches led to the borophagine and canine <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_radiation" title="Evolutionary radiation">radiations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gittleman_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gittleman-19">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miocene_epoch">Miocene epoch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Miocene epoch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Around 9–10 Mya during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Miocene" title="Late Miocene">Late Miocene</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Canis" title="Canis">Canis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Urocyon" title="Urocyon">Urocyon</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Vulpes" title="Vulpes">Vulpes</a></i> genera expanded from southwestern <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, where the canine radiation began. The success of these canines was related to the development of lower <a href="/wiki/Carnassial" title="Carnassial">carnassials</a> that were capable of both <a href="/wiki/Mastication" class="mw-redirect" title="Mastication">mastication</a> and shearing.<sup id="cite_ref-Gittleman_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gittleman-19">[19]</a></sup> Around 8 Mya, the <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringian</a> land bridge allowed members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Eucyon" title="Eucyon">Eucyon</a></i> a means to enter Asia and they continued on to colonize Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Perini_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perini-20">[20]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pliocene_epoch">Pliocene epoch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pliocene epoch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a>, around 4–5 Mya, <i><a href="/wiki/Canis_lepophagus" title="Canis lepophagus">Canis lepophagus</a></i> appeared in North America. This was small and sometimes coyote-like. Others were wolf-like in characteristics. <i>C. latrans</i> (the coyote) is theorized to have descended from <i>C. lepophagus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The formation of the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a>, about 3 Mya, joined <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> to North America, allowing canids to <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Interchange" title="Great American Interchange">invade South America</a>, where they diversified. However, the most recent common ancestor of the South American canids lived in North America some 4 Mya and more than one incursion across the new land bridge is likely. One of the resulting lineages consisted of the <a href="/wiki/Gray_fox" title="Gray fox">gray fox</a> (<i>Urocyon cinereoargentus</i>) and the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Dire_wolf" title="Dire wolf">dire wolf</a> (<i>Canis dirus</i>). The other lineage consisted of the so-called South American endemic species; the maned wolf (<i>Chrysocyon brachyurus</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_dog" title="Short-eared dog">short-eared dog</a> (<i>Atelocynus microtis</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Bush_dog" title="Bush dog">bush dog</a> (<i>Speothos venaticus</i>), the crab-eating fox (<i>Cerdocyon thous</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/South_American_fox" title="South American fox">South American foxes</a> (<i>Lycalopex</i> spp.). The monophyly of this group has been established by molecular means.<sup id="cite_ref-Perini_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perini-20">[20]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pleistocene_epoch">Pleistocene epoch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pleistocene epoch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Borophagus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Borophagus.jpg/220px-Borophagus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Borophagus.jpg/330px-Borophagus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Borophagus.jpg/440px-Borophagus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2872" data-file-height="1653"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Borophagus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Restoration of two 
         <i>Borophagus</i> sp. preying on a 
         <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, the North American wolf line appeared, with <i><a href="/wiki/Canis_edwardii" title="Canis edwardii">Canis edwardii</a></i>, clearly identifiable as a wolf, and <i><a href="/wiki/Canis_rufus" class="mw-redirect" title="Canis rufus">Canis rufus</a></i> appeared, possibly a direct descendant of <i>C. edwardii</i>. Around 0.8 Mya, <i><a href="/wiki/Canis_ambrusteri" class="mw-redirect" title="Canis ambrusteri">Canis ambrusteri</a></i> emerged in North America. A large wolf, it was found all over North and Central America and was eventually supplanted by its descendant, the dire wolf, which then spread into South America during the late Pleistocene.<sup id="cite_ref-Larson_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Larson-22">[22]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 0.3 Mya, a number of subspecies of the gray wolf (<i>C. lupus</i>) had developed and had spread throughout Europe and northern Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The gray wolf colonized North America during the late <a href="/wiki/Rancholabrean" title="Rancholabrean">Rancholabrean</a> era across the Bering land bridge, with at least three separate invasions, with each one consisting of one or more different Eurasian gray wolf clades.<sup id="cite_ref-Chambers_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers-24">[24]</a></sup> MtDNA studies have shown that there are at least four extant <i>C. lupus</i> lineages.<sup id="cite_ref-gaubert_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaubert-25">[25]</a></sup> The dire wolf shared its habitat with the gray wolf, but became extinct in a large-scale extinction event that occurred around 11,500 years ago. It may have been more of a scavenger than a hunter; its molars appear to be adapted for crushing bones and it may have gone extinct as a result of the extinction of the large herbivorous animals on whose carcasses it relied.<sup id="cite_ref-Larson_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Larson-22">[22]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2015, a study of mitochondrial genome sequences and whole genome nuclear sequences of African and Eurasian canids indicated that extant wolf-like canids have colonized Africa from Eurasia at least five times throughout the Pliocene and Pleistocene, which is consistent with fossil evidence suggesting that much of African canid fauna diversity resulted from the immigration of Eurasian ancestors, likely coincident with Plio-Pleistocene climatic oscillations between arid and humid conditions. When comparing the African and Eurasian golden jackals, the study concluded that the African specimens represented a distinct monophyletic lineage that should be recognized as a separate species, <i><a href="/wiki/Canis_anthus" class="mw-redirect" title="Canis anthus">Canis anthus</a></i> (African golden wolf). According to a phylogeny derived from nuclear sequences, the Eurasian golden jackal (<i>Canis aureus</i>) diverged from the wolf/coyote lineage 1.9 Mya, but the African golden wolf separated 1.3 Mya. Mitochondrial genome sequences indicated the Ethiopian wolf diverged from the wolf/coyote lineage slightly prior to that.<sup id="cite_ref-Koepfli-2015_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koepfli-2015-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>S1</span></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics"><span id="Anatomy"></span><span id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P1a_-_Canis_lupus,_Canis_aureus_%26_Cuon_alpinus_paws.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/MSU_V2P1a_-_Canis_lupus%2C_Canis_aureus_%26_Cuon_alpinus_paws.png/220px-MSU_V2P1a_-_Canis_lupus%2C_Canis_aureus_%26_Cuon_alpinus_paws.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/MSU_V2P1a_-_Canis_lupus%2C_Canis_aureus_%26_Cuon_alpinus_paws.png/330px-MSU_V2P1a_-_Canis_lupus%2C_Canis_aureus_%26_Cuon_alpinus_paws.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/MSU_V2P1a_-_Canis_lupus%2C_Canis_aureus_%26_Cuon_alpinus_paws.png 2x" data-file-width="405" data-file-height="225"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:MSU_V2P1a_-_Canis_lupus,_Canis_aureus_%26_Cuon_alpinus_paws.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Comparative illustration of the paws of 
         <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">gray wolf</a>, 
         <a href="/wiki/Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal">golden jackal</a>, and 
         <a href="/wiki/Dhole" title="Dhole">dhole</a> by A. N. Komarov
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Wild canids are found on every continent except Antarctica, and inhabit a wide range of different habitats, including <a href="/wiki/Deserts" class="mw-redirect" title="Deserts">deserts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountains">mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forests" class="mw-redirect" title="Forests">forests</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a>. They vary in size from the fennec fox, which may be as little as 24&nbsp;cm (9.4&nbsp;in) in length and weigh 0.6&nbsp;kg (1.3&nbsp;lb),<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> to the <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">gray wolf</a>, which may be up to 160&nbsp;cm (5.2&nbsp;ft) long, and can weigh up to 79&nbsp;kg (174&nbsp;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-heptner1998_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heptner1998-27">[27]</a></sup> Only a few species are <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">arboreal</a> – the gray fox, the closely related island fox<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and the raccoon dog habitually climb trees.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>All canids have a similar basic form, as exemplified by the grey wolf, although the relative length of muzzle, limbs, ears and tail vary considerably between species. With the exceptions of the bush dog, the raccoon dog and some <a href="/wiki/Dog_breed" title="Dog breed">domestic breeds of <i>Canis lupus</i></a>, canids have relatively long legs and lithe bodies, adapted for chasing prey. The tails are bushy and the length and quality of the pelage varies with the season. The muzzle portion of the skull is much more elongated than that of the cat family. The <a href="/wiki/Zygomatic_arch" title="Zygomatic arch">zygomatic arches</a> are wide, there is a transverse <a href="/wiki/Lambdoidal" class="mw-redirect" title="Lambdoidal">lambdoidal</a> ridge at the rear of the <a href="/wiki/Cranium" class="mw-redirect" title="Cranium">cranium</a> and in some species, a <a href="/wiki/Sagittal_crest" title="Sagittal crest">sagittal crest</a> running from front to back. The bony orbits around the eye never form a complete ring and the auditory bullae are smooth and rounded.<sup id="cite_ref-Mivart_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mivart-32">[32]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg/220px-Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg/330px-Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg/440px-Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Black-backed_Jackal_skeleton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Skeleton of a black-backed jackal (
         <a href="/wiki/Black-backed_jackal" title="Black-backed jackal"><i>Canis mesomelos</i></a> on display at the 
         <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Osteology" title="Museum of Osteology">Museum of Osteology</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>All canids are <a href="/wiki/Digitigrade" title="Digitigrade">digitigrade</a>, meaning they walk on their toes. The <a href="/wiki/Rhinarium" title="Rhinarium">tip of the nose</a> is always naked, as are the cushioned pads on the soles of the feet. These latter consist of a single pad behind the tip of each toe and a more-or-less three-lobed central pad under the roots of the digits. Hairs grow between the pads and in the Arctic fox, the sole of the foot is densely covered with hair at some times of year. With the exception of the four-toed African wild dog (<i>Lycaon pictus</i>), five toes are on the forefeet, but the pollex (thumb) is reduced and does not reach the ground. On the hind feet are four toes, but in some domestic dogs, a fifth vestigial toe, known as a <a href="/wiki/Dewclaw" title="Dewclaw">dewclaw</a>, is sometimes present, but has no anatomical connection to the rest of the foot. The slightly curved nails are not retractile and more-or-less blunt.<sup id="cite_ref-Mivart_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mivart-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Canine_penis" title="Canine penis">The penis in male canids</a> is supported by a bone called the <a href="/wiki/Baculum" title="Baculum">baculum</a>. It also contains a structure at the base called the <a href="/wiki/Bulbus_glandis" title="Bulbus glandis">bulbus glandis</a>, which helps to create a <a href="/wiki/Copulatory_tie" class="mw-redirect" title="Copulatory tie">copulatory tie</a> during mating, locking the animals together for up to an hour.<sup id="cite_ref-Ewer1973_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ewer1973-33">[33]</a></sup> Young canids are born blind, with their eyes opening a few weeks after birth.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> All living canids (Caninae) have a ligament analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Nuchal_ligament" title="Nuchal ligament">nuchal ligament</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ungulates" class="mw-redirect" title="Ungulates">ungulates</a> used to maintain the posture of the head and neck with little active muscle exertion; this ligament allows them to conserve energy while running long distances following scent trails with their nose to the ground. However, based on skeletal details of the neck, at least some of the Borophaginae (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Aelurodon" title="Aelurodon">Aelurodon</a></i>) are believed to have lacked this ligament.<sup id="cite_ref-wang2008_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wang2008-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>97–98</span></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dentition">Dentition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dentition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg/300px-Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg/450px-Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg/600px-Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Wolf_cranium_labelled.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Diagram of a wolf skull with key features labelled
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lupocranio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/Lupocranio.jpg/220px-Lupocranio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/Lupocranio.jpg/330px-Lupocranio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/Lupocranio.jpg/440px-Lupocranio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="979" data-file-height="734"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lupocranio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_wolf" title="Eurasian wolf">Eurasian wolf</a> skull
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Dentition" title="Dentition">Dentition</a> relates to the arrangement of teeth in the mouth, with the <a href="/wiki/Dentition#Dental_formula" title="Dentition">dental notation</a> for the upper-jaw teeth using the upper-case letters I to denote <a href="/wiki/Incisors" class="mw-redirect" title="Incisors">incisors</a>, C for <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canines</a>, P for <a href="/wiki/Premolars" class="mw-redirect" title="Premolars">premolars</a>, and M for <a href="/wiki/Molars" class="mw-redirect" title="Molars">molars</a>, and the lower-case letters i, c, p and m to denote the <a href="/wiki/Mandible" title="Mandible">mandible teeth</a>. Teeth are numbered using one side of the mouth and from the front of the mouth to the back. In <a href="/wiki/Carnivores" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnivores">carnivores</a>, the upper premolar P4 and the lower molar m1 form the <a href="/wiki/Carnassials" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnassials">carnassials</a> that are used together in a scissor-like action to shear the muscle and tendon of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-wang2008_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wang2008-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>74</span></sup> </p>
      <p>Canids use their premolars for cutting and crushing except for the upper fourth premolar P4 (the upper carnassial) that is only used for cutting. They use their molars for grinding except for the lower first molar m1 (the lower carnassial) that has evolved for both cutting and grinding depending on the candid's dietary adaptation. On the lower carnassial, the <a href="/wiki/Trigonid" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonid">trigonid</a> is used for slicing and the <a href="/wiki/Talonid" class="mw-redirect" title="Talonid">talonid</a> is used for grinding. The ratio between the trigonid and the talonid indicates a carnivore's dietary habits, with a larger trigonid indicating a <a href="/wiki/Hypercarnivore" title="Hypercarnivore">hypercarnivore</a> and a larger talonid indicating a more <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivorous</a> diet.<sup id="cite_ref-sansalone2015_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sansalone2015-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cherin2013_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cherin2013-37">[37]</a></sup> Because of its low variability, the length of the lower carnassial is used to provide an estimate of a carnivore's body size.<sup id="cite_ref-sansalone2015_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sansalone2015-36">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A study of the estimated bite force at the canine teeth of a large sample of living and fossil mammalian predators, when adjusted for their body mass, found that for <a href="/wiki/Placental" class="mw-redirect" title="Placental">placental</a> mammals the bite force at the canines was greatest in the extinct dire wolf (163), followed among the modern canids by the four hypercarnivores that often prey on animals larger than themselves: the African wild dog (142), the gray wolf (136), the dhole (112), and the dingo (108). The bite force at the carnassials showed a similar trend to the canines. A predator's largest prey size is strongly influenced by its biomechanical limits.<sup id="cite_ref-wroe2005_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wroe2005-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Most canids have 42 <a href="/wiki/Tooth" title="Tooth">teeth</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Dentition" title="Dentition">dental formula</a> of: <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.4.2</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.4.3</span></span></span>. The bush dog has only one upper molar with two below, the dhole has two above and two below. and the bat-eared fox has three or four upper molars and four lower ones.<sup id="cite_ref-Mivart_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mivart-32">[32]</a></sup> The molar teeth are strong in most species, allowing the animals to crack open bone to reach the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">marrow</a>. The deciduous, or baby teeth, formula in canids is <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.3</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">3.1.3</span></span></span>, molars being completely absent.<sup id="cite_ref-Mivart_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mivart-32">[32]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_history">Life history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Life history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_behavior">Social behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Social behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg/220px-2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg/330px-2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg/440px-2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="591" data-file-height="368"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:2012-bandipur-dhole-sambar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Dhole" title="Dhole">Dholes</a> attacking a 
         <a href="/wiki/Sambar_(deer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sambar (deer)">sambar</a>, 
         <a href="/wiki/Bandipur_National_Park" title="Bandipur National Park">Bandipur National Park</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: 
       <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf#Behaviour" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">Gray wolf §&nbsp;Behaviour</a>, 
       <a href="/wiki/Dog_behavior" title="Dog behavior">Dog behavior</a>, 
       <a href="/wiki/Red_fox#Behaviour" title="Red fox">Red fox §&nbsp;Behaviour</a>, and 
       <a href="/wiki/African_wild_dog#Behaviour" title="African wild dog">African wild dog §&nbsp;Behaviour</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Almost all canids are social animals and live together in groups. In general, they are territorial or have a home range and sleep in the open, using their dens only for breeding and sometimes in bad weather.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In most foxes, and in many of the true dogs, a <a href="/wiki/Breeding_pair" title="Breeding pair">male and female pair</a> work together to hunt and to raise their young. Gray wolves and some of the other larger canids live in larger groups called <a href="/wiki/Pack_(canine)" title="Pack (canine)">packs</a>. African wild dogs have packs which may consist of 20 to 40 animals and packs of fewer than about seven individuals may be incapable of successful reproduction.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Hunting in packs has the advantage that larger prey items can be tackled. Some species form packs or live in small family groups depending on the circumstances, including the type of available food. In most species, some individuals live on their own. Within a canid pack, there is a system of dominance so that the strongest, most experienced animals lead the pack. In most cases, the dominant male and female are the only pack members to breed.<sup id="cite_ref-ADW_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADW-41">[41]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px">
         <audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="42.854943310658" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Red_Fox_(Vulpes_vulpes)_(W1CDR0001529_BD12).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons">
          <source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/79/Red_Fox_%28Vulpes_vulpes%29_%28W1CDR0001529_BD12%29.ogg/Red_Fox_%28Vulpes_vulpes%29_%28W1CDR0001529_BD12%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="128864">
          <source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Red_Fox_%28Vulpes_vulpes%29_%28W1CDR0001529_BD12%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (213 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="213148">
         </audio>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Red_Fox_(Vulpes_vulpes)_(W1CDR0001529_BD12).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Red foxes barking in Pinbury Park, Gloucestershire, England.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Canids communicate with each other by <a href="/wiki/Scent_marking" class="mw-redirect" title="Scent marking">scent signals</a>, by visual clues and gestures, and by vocalizations such as growls, barks and howls. In most cases, groups have a home territory from which they drive out other <a href="/wiki/Conspecific" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspecific">conspecifics</a>. The territory is <a href="/wiki/Raised-leg_urination" class="mw-redirect" title="Raised-leg urination">marked by leaving urine scent marks</a>, which warn trespassing individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Social behaviour is also mediated by secretions from glands on the upper surface of the tail near its root and from the <a href="/wiki/Anal_gland" title="Anal gland">anal glands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ADW_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADW-41">[41]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Canine_reproduction" title="Canine reproduction">Canine reproduction</a>
      </div> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047399">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Korean_wolves_mating_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Korean_wolves_mating_%28cropped%29.jpg/200px-Korean_wolves_mating_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="127" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Korean_wolves_mating_%28cropped%29.jpg/300px-Korean_wolves_mating_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Korean_wolves_mating_%28cropped%29.jpg/400px-Korean_wolves_mating_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2392" data-file-height="1523"></a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Red_foxes_mating_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Red_foxes_mating_%282%29.jpg/200px-Red_foxes_mating_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="138" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Red_foxes_mating_%282%29.jpg/300px-Red_foxes_mating_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Red_foxes_mating_%282%29.jpg/400px-Red_foxes_mating_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="830" data-file-height="573"></a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left"></div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
         <a href="/wiki/Wolves_mating" class="mw-redirect" title="Wolves mating">Gray wolves</a> and 
         <a href="/wiki/Red_fox#Reproduction_and_development" title="Red fox">red foxes mating</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg/220px-Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg/330px-Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg/440px-Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1502" data-file-height="1038"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Wilde_huendin_am_stillen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A feral dog from Sri Lanka nursing her puppies
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Canids as a group exhibit several reproductive traits that are uncommon among mammals as a whole. They are typically <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamous</a>, provide paternal care to their offspring, have reproductive cycles with lengthy <a href="/wiki/Estrous_cycle#Proestrus" title="Estrous cycle">proestral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estrous_cycle#Metestrus_or_diestrus" title="Estrous cycle">dioestral</a> phases and have a copulatory tie during mating. They also retain adult offspring in the social group, suppressing the ability of these to breed while making use of the <a href="/wiki/Alloparenting" title="Alloparenting">alloparental care</a> they can provide to help raise the next generation of offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-Asa_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asa-43">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the proestral period, increased levels of <a href="/wiki/Oestradiol" class="mw-redirect" title="Oestradiol">oestradiol</a> make the female attractive to the male. There is a rise in <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">progesterone</a> during the oestral phase and the female is now receptive. Following this, the level of oestradiol fluctuates and there is a lengthy dioestrous phase during which the female is pregnant. Pseudo-pregnancy frequently occurs in canids that have ovulated but failed to conceive. A period of <a href="/wiki/Estrous_cycle#anestrus" title="Estrous cycle">anoestrus</a> follows pregnancy or <a href="/wiki/False_pregnancy" title="False pregnancy">pseudo-pregnancy</a>, there being only one oestral period during each breeding season. Small and medium-sized canids mostly have a <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a> period of 50 to 60 days, while larger species average 60 to 65 days. The time of year in which the breeding season occurs is related to the length of day, as has been demonstrated in the case of several species that have been translocated across the equator to the other hemisphere and experiences a six-month shift of phase. Domestic dogs and certain small canids in captivity may come into oestrus more frequently, perhaps because the <a href="/wiki/Photoperiod" class="mw-redirect" title="Photoperiod">photoperiod</a> stimulus breaks down under conditions of artificial lighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Asa_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Asa-43">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The size of a litter varies, with from one to 16 or more pups being born. The young are born small, blind and helpless and require a long period of parental care. They are kept in a den, most often dug into the ground, for warmth and protection.<sup id="cite_ref-Mivart_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mivart-32">[32]</a></sup> When the young begin eating solid food, both parents, and often other pack members, bring food back for them from the hunt. This is most often vomited up from the adult's stomach. Where such pack involvement in the feeding of the litter occurs, the breeding success rate is higher than is the case where females split from the group and rear their pups in isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Young canids may take a year to mature and learn the skills they need to survive.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In some species, such as the African wild dog, male offspring usually remain in the natal pack, while females disperse as a group and join another small group of the opposite sex to form a new pack.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Canids_and_humans">Canids and humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Canids and humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:BedaleHunt2005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BedaleHunt2005.jpg/220px-BedaleHunt2005.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BedaleHunt2005.jpg/330px-BedaleHunt2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BedaleHunt2005.jpg/440px-BedaleHunt2005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="581"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:BedaleHunt2005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Traditional English 
         <a href="/wiki/Fox_hunt" class="mw-redirect" title="Fox hunt">fox hunt</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: 
       <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">Dog</a>
      </div> 
      <p>One canid, the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_domestic_dog" title="Origin of the domestic dog">domestic dog</a>, entered into a partnership with humans a long time ago. The archaeological record shows the first undisputed dog remains buried beside humans 14,700 years ago,<sup id="cite_ref-giemsch2015_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giemsch2015-47">[47]</a></sup> with disputed remains occurring 36,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-germonpre2009_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-germonpre2009-48">[48]</a></sup> These dates imply that the earliest dogs arose in the time of human <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a> and not <a href="/wiki/Agriculturists" class="mw-redirect" title="Agriculturists">agriculturists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thalmann2013_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thalmann2013-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-freedman2014_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedman2014-50">[50]</a></sup> The dog was the first domesticated species.<sup id="cite_ref-larson2014_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-larson2012_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larson2012-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-britannica_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-perri2016_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perri2016-54">[54]</a></sup> The fact that wolves are pack animals with cooperative social structures may have been the reason that the relationship developed. Humans benefited from the canid's loyalty, cooperation, teamwork, alertness and tracking abilities, while the wolf may have benefited from the use of weapons to tackle larger prey and the sharing of food. Humans and dogs may have evolved together.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Among canids, only the gray wolf has widely been known to prey on humans.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Nonetheless, at least <a href="/wiki/Coyote_attacks_on_humans" class="mw-redirect" title="Coyote attacks on humans">two records</a> have coyotes killing humans,<sup id="cite_ref-AOH_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOH-57">[57]</a></sup> and two have golden jackals killing children.<sup id="cite_ref-CWC_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CWC-58">[58]</a></sup> Human beings have trapped and hunted some canid species for their fur and, especially the gray wolf, the coyote and the red fox, for sport.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Canids such as the <a href="/wiki/Dhole" title="Dhole">dhole</a> are now <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> in the wild because of persecution, habitat loss, a depletion of ungulate prey species and transmission of diseases from domestic dogs.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extant_and_recently_extinct_species">Extant and recently extinct species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Extant and recently extinct species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

      <p>All extant species of family Canidae are in subfamily Caninae. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subfamily_Caninae">Subfamily Caninae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Subfamily Caninae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <ul>
       <li><b>True dogs</b> – Tribe Canini 
        <ul>
         <li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Canis" title="Canis">Canis</a></i> (see also <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canis_species_and_subspecies" title="List of Canis species and subspecies">List of Canis species and subspecies</a> which also includes some varieties) 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">Gray wolf</a>, <i>Canis lupus</i> (2.723 Mya to present) 
            <ul>
             <li><a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">Domestic dog</a>, <i>Canis lupus familiaris</i></li> 
             <li><a href="/wiki/Dingo" title="Dingo">Dingo</a>, most often classified as <i>Canis lupus dingo</i></li> 
             <li><a href="/wiki/Subspecies_of_Canis_lupus" title="Subspecies of Canis lupus">Many other subspecies</a></li>
            </ul></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Red_wolf" title="Red wolf">Red wolf</a>, <i>Canis rufus</i> (sometimes considered a subspecies of the gray wolf, but including several subtaxa of its own, including the <a href="/wiki/Florida_black_wolf" title="Florida black wolf">Florida black wolf</a>)</li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_wolf" title="Eastern wolf">Eastern wolf</a>, <i>Canis lycaon</i></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">Coyote</a>, <i>Canis latrans</i> (also called prairie wolf and brush wolf)</li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Dire_wolf" title="Dire wolf">Dire wolf</a>, <i>Canis dirus</i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=0.25">0.25</a> mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/African_golden_wolf" title="African golden wolf">African golden wolf</a>, <i>Canis anthus</i></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Golden_jackal" title="Golden jackal">Eurasian golden jackal</a>, <i>Canis aureus</i></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_wolf" title="Ethiopian wolf">Ethiopian wolf</a>, <i>Canis simensis</i> (also called Abyssinian red jackal, Simien fox and Simien jackal)</li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Side-striped_jackal" title="Side-striped jackal">Side-striped jackal</a>, <i>Canis adustus</i></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Black-backed_jackal" title="Black-backed jackal">Black-backed jackal</a>, <i>Canis mesomelas</i> (also called silver-backed jackal)</li>
          </ul></li> 
         <li>Genus <i>Cuon</i> 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/Dhole" title="Dhole">Dhole</a>, <i>Cuon alpinus</i> or <i>Canis alpinus</i> (also called Asiatic wild dog)</li>
          </ul></li> 
         <li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Cynotherium" class="mw-redirect" title="Cynotherium">Cynotherium</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a> 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/Sardinian_dhole" title="Sardinian dhole">Sardinian dhole</a>, <i>Cynotherium sardous</i> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li>
          </ul></li> 
         <li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Lycaon_(genus)" title="Lycaon (genus)">Lycaon</a></i> 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/African_wild_dog" title="African wild dog">African wild dog</a>, <i>Lycaon pictus</i> (also called African hunting dog)</li>
          </ul></li> 
         <li>Genus <i>Atelocynus</i> 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/Short-eared_dog" title="Short-eared dog">Short-eared dog</a>, <i>Atelocynus microtis</i></li>
          </ul></li> 
         <li>Genus <i>Cerdocyon</i> 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/Crab-eating_fox" title="Crab-eating fox">Crab-eating fox</a>, <i>Cerdocyon thous</i></li>
          </ul></li> 
         <li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Dusicyon" title="Dusicyon">Dusicyon</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a> 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/Falkland_Island_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Falkland Island wolf">Falkland Island wolf</a>, <i>Dusicyon australis</i> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li> 
           <li><i><a href="/wiki/Dusicyon_avus" title="Dusicyon avus">Dusicyon avus</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li>
          </ul></li> 
         <li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Lycalopex" class="mw-redirect" title="Lycalopex">Lycalopex</a> (Pseudalopex)</i> 
          <ul>
           <li><a href="/wiki/Culpeo" title="Culpeo">Culpeo</a>, <i>Lycalopex culpaeus</i> 
            <ul>
             <li><a href="/wiki/Fuegian_dog" title="Fuegian dog">Fuegian dog</a>, <i>Lycalopex culpaeus domesticus</i> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li>
            </ul></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Darwin%27s_fox" title="Darwin's fox">Darwin's fox</a>, <i>Lycalopex fulvipes</i></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/South_American_gray_fox" title="South American gray fox">South American gray fox</a>, <i>Lycalopex griseus</i></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Pampas_fox" title="Pampas fox">Pampas fox</a>, <i>Lycalopex gymnocercus</i></li> 
           <li><a href="/wiki/Sechuran_fox" title="Sechuran fox">Sechuran fox</a>, <i>Lycalopex sechurae</i></li>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_Canidae">Prehistoric Canidae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Prehistoric Canidae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Except where otherwise stated, the following classification is based on a 1994 paper by <a href="/wiki/Xiaoming_Wang_(paleontologist)" title="Xiaoming Wang (paleontologist)">Xiaoming Wang</a>, curator of terrestrial mammals at the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum_of_Los_Angeles_County" title="Natural History Museum of Los Angeles County">Natural History Museum of Los Angeles County</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">systematics</a> of the subfamily <a href="/wiki/Hesperocyoninae" title="Hesperocyoninae">Hesperocyoninae</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Hesperocyoninae_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hesperocyoninae-61">[61]</a></sup> a 1999 paper by Wang, together with the zoologists <a href="/wiki/Richard_H._Tedford" title="Richard H. Tedford">Richard H. Tedford</a> and Beryl E. Taylor on the subfamily <a href="/wiki/Borophaginae" title="Borophaginae">Borophaginae</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Borophaginae_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borophaginae-62">[62]</a></sup> and a 2009 paper by Tedford, Wang and Taylor on the North American fossil Caninae.<sup id="cite_ref-Tedford_2009_1–218_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tedford_2009_1–218-63">[63]</a></sup> </p>

      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subfamily_Borophaginae">Subfamily <a href="/wiki/Borophaginae" title="Borophaginae">Borophaginae</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Subfamily Borophaginae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <ul>
       <li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeocyon" title="Archaeocyon">Archaeocyon</a></i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=33.3-26.3">7</a> Mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a>

         <li><i>Archaeocyon falkenbachi</i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=30.8-20.6">10.2</a> Mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li> 
         <li><i>Archaeocyon leptodus</i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=33.3-26.3">7</a> Mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li> 
         <li><i>Archaeocyon pavidus</i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=33.3-26.3">7</a> Mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li>
        </ul></li>

        <ul>
         <li><i>Otarocyon cooki</i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=30.8-26.3">4.5</a> Mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li>

             <li><i><a href="/wiki/Borophagus_pugnator" title="Borophagus pugnator">Borophagus pugnator</a></i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=13.6-5.3">8.3</a> Mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li> 
             <li><i><a href="/wiki/Borophagus_secundus" title="Borophagus secundus">Borophagus secundus</a></i> (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=13.6-5.3">8.3</a> Mya</span>) <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a></li>
            </ul></li>
          </ul></li>
        </ul></li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subfamily_Hesperocyoninae">Subfamily <a href="/wiki/Hesperocyoninae" title="Hesperocyoninae">Hesperocyoninae</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canidae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Subfamily Hesperocyoninae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">†</a> (Mya = million years ago) </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Cynodesmus" title="Cynodesmus">Cynodesmus</a></i> (32–29 Mya)
